            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="db1d5be68d3241b1b2a8e7e8618237cf">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f531ed6f-b2d5-4435-b625-f535b88574f4'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d5642997" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Monolayer atomic crystals show significant advantages in improving charge storage kinetics for electrode materials. While notable progress is made, challenges remain in producing nanocrystals with desirable configurations, dimensions, and crystallographic properties. Here, 1D single-crystal nanobelts assembled from monolayer sodium titanate nanobelts are reported with highly exposed active sites as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). The unique structural properties of the 1D single-crystal nanobelts offer excellent electrochemical activity, electrochemo-mechanical stability, and well-maintained structural integrity, leading to highly efficient sodium ion storage performance. Insights into the electrochemical reaction processes, as revealed by in situ transmission electron microscopy, in situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction, and theoretical calculations, indicate that the 1D single-crystal nanobelts enable favorable sodium ion storage kinetics and a low-strain characteristic. This facilitates fast charge/discharge capability and long-term cycling stability for up to 5000 cycles at 20&nbsp;C. Moreover, the 1D single-crystal nanobelts demonstrate practical applicability. A pouch cell assembled with the 1D single-crystal nanobelts anode and iron-based Prussian blue cathode exhibits highly stable cycling, achieving a low capacity fading ratio of ≈0.05% per cycle over 150 cycles. This study provides an innovative design principle to enhance the charge storage capability of electrode materials through intelligent structural nanoengineering.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400929-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400929-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The rapid expansion of the market for portable electronic devices, electric vehicles, and grid energy storage has driven the development of various rechargeable batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0001_R_d5642989e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Nat. Energy Nat. Energy Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nano-Micro Lett. Rare Met. J. Mater. Chem. A.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are a promising solution for advancing rechargeable battery technologies beyond lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to their cost-effectiveness, sustainability in their raw materials, and high safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0002_R_d5642989e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Chem. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Importantly, SIBs share similar working principles and configurations with the LIBs. Therefore, the fundamental knowledge and production technology techniques developed for LIBs can be adopted for SIBs. However, the energy density, cycling life, and fast charge/discharge capability of SIBs are not satisfactory.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0001_R_d5642989e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Nat. Energy Nat. Energy Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nano-Micro Lett. Rare Met. J. Mater. Chem. A.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0003_R_d5642989e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Graphite, the preferred anode for LIBs, shows limited Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity when used as an anode for SIBs. Therefore, alternative, reliable anode materials are required for SIBs. Several anode materials with a high theoretical capacity have been developed for SIBs, such as metal/alloys,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0004_R_d5642989e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Nano Lett. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> metal dichalcogenides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0005_R_d5642989e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and phosphorous/phosphides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0006_R_d5642989e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Res.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these materials face severe challenges, such as significant volume expansion upon Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation, which leads to limited electrochemical reversibility and stability. Sodium titanate has received significant research interest due to its eco-friendliness, abundant raw materials, and minor structural change upon charging and discharging. Additionally, sodium titanate exhibits a predominant sodiation voltage of ≈0.3&nbsp;V, which is significantly higher than that of most anode candidates, such as hard carbon. This characteristic mitigates the risk of hazardous sodium metal plating, thereby ensuring enhanced battery safety during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0007_R_d5642989e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, sodium titanate anodes face critical challenges, including sluggish electrochemical kinetics, low capacity, and inferior cycling cyclability, which limit utility in SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The fabrication of nanostructures including hollow/yolk-shell structures,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0004_R_d5642989e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Nano Lett. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0005_R_d5642989e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0008_R_d5642989e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> nanosheets,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0007_R_d5642989e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a>, <a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0009_R_d5642989e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> porous structures,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0005_R_d5642989e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0010_R_d5642989e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and hierarchical configurations,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0005_R_d5642989e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0011_R_d5642989e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> has been demonstrated as a promising strategy for reducing electron and Na<sup>+</sup> migration distances, thereby enhancing the electrochemical dynamics of battery electrode materials. Specifically, nanocrystals with dimensions at the atomic scale exhibit a maximized atomic interface, highly exposed active sites, and reduced solid-state ion diffusion distances. These benefits then manifest in charge storage capability as reversible capacity, fast de-/intercalation kinetics, and electrochemical reversibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0012_R_d5642989e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy Chem. Soc. Rev.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While significant progress has been made in synthesizing atomic nanocrystals through chemical exfoliation methods, challenges remain in achieving nanocrystals with the configurations, dimensions, and crystallographic properties desirable for battery applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0013_R_d5642989e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Rev. Nat. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the synthesis of ultrathin atomic nanocrystals via exfoliation often results in 2D geometry. These 2D nanocrystals face significant challenges related to aggregation and structure degradation, particularly during material synthesis and subsequent electrode fabrication processes. These issues adversely affect electrochemical performance by reducing the number of active sites and generating complex ion transport paths. Furthermore, atomic nanocrystals with specific crystallographic features, such as exposed crystal faces, offer a promising avenue for enhancing the electrochemical performance of electrode materials due to their increased electrochemical activity resulting from favorable interface kinetics. However, such studies have rarely been reported in the research on sodium titanate anodes for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we present the design and synthesis of ultralong 1D, single-crystal nanobelts (denoted as 1D SCNs) composed of self-assembled monolayer sodium titanate nanobelts (denoted as mNTBs) that feature highly exposed crystal planes as SIB anode materials, as illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1a</a>. The 1D SCNs possess unique crystallographic and morphological features that enable them to exhibit noteworthy characteristics, including a) a large interlayer distance between the monolayer nanobelts that helps to mitigate lattice volume expansion induced by Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation, thus achieving a low-strain characteristic; b) abundant electrochemically active sites resulting from the highly exposed planes in the monolayer nanobelts for storing Na<sup>+</sup>, providing additional capacity and enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics; c) high mechanical strength, ensuring microstructural integrity of the nanobelts during cycling, preventing degradation and capacity fading over long-term cycling; and d) a 1D self-assembly configuration to effectively mitigate aggregation, enhancing electrochemical stability. By coupling the 1D SCNs with carbon nanotubes (CNTs), flexible, free-standing electrodes were fabricated, demonstrating excellent battery performance even at 0&nbsp;°C. Kinetics analysis reveals a prominent Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation pseudocapacitive charge storage mechanism that yields high-rate capability. Theoretical calculations indicate that the atomic interface contact between the 1D SCNs and CNTs significantly enhances the interface charge transfer reaction kinetics. In situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and in situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction (XRD) techniques reveal that the structural integrity of the 1D SCNs leads to a low-strain characteristic during Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/de-intercalation. This translates into exceptional long-term cycling stability with a capacity retention of 88% over 5000 cycles at 20 C. Furthermore, the assembled pouch cell with an iron-based Prussian blue analog cathode demonstrates stable cycling with a low capacity fading ratio of ≈0.05% per cycle and a Coulombic efficiency of ≈99% over 150 cycles, making the 1D SCNs a promising anode candidate for practical applications.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400929-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/696fd828-dbed-4bc4-9313-5d912940a6f4/aenm202400929-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/696fd828-dbed-4bc4-9313-5d912940a6f4/aenm202400929-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/66d321fd-c34f-4d23-91af-4e82523390a2/aenm202400929-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/696fd828-dbed-4bc4-9313-5d912940a6f4/aenm202400929-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterizations. a) Structural illustration of the 1D SCNs, mNTB, and (001) base plane of the mNTB; b) SEM image, c) top view TEM image, d) AFM analysis, e) side view TEM image, and f) side view HADDF image of the 1D SCNs; g) Top view HADDF image of the mNTB. h) EDS mapping, i) Raman spectrum, and j) Ti 2p XPS spectrum of the 1D SCNs.</div>

      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400929-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400929-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The XRD pattern of the 1D SCNs is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), where the diffraction peaks are consistent with the typical sodium titanate phase of NaTi<sub>1.25</sub>O<sub>3</sub> based on Rietveld refinement and previously studies,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0014_R_d5642989e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Acta Crystallogr. B Adv. Sci.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicating that a pure phase material was obtained. The refinement results reveal that the 1D SCNs are in the monoclinic space group <i>C 1 2/m 1</i>, with lattice parameters of <i>a</i> = 20.41947(7) Å, <i>b</i> = 3.92769(4) Å, and <i>c</i> = 11.52914(0) Å. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and TEM images demonstrate a well-developed 1D nanobelt configuration featuring a length of ≈30&nbsp;µm and a width of 100–200&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1b,c</a>; Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S4</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern confirms a single-crystalline nature, showing a set of sharp diffraction spots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Atomic force microscopy (AFM) analysis reveals a plateau profile with a topographic height ≈28&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1d</a>). Side view TEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1e</a>) and high-angle annular dark-filed (HAADF) STEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1f</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) images show that the 1D SCNs have a layered stacking order and are assembled on the monolayer nanobelts of mNTO with an average interlayer distance of 7.52 Å. A planar HAADF study of a monolayer nanobelt (mNTB) shows that the (001) plane of the mNTO has a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.84 nm and is parallel to the longitudinal direction of the nanobelts (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1g</a>). This orientation allows the edge sites of the (001) base plane to be exposed for storing Na<sup>+</sup> ions, as illustrated in the structural models in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1a</a>. Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping confirms that the Na, Ti, and O elements are uniformly distributed throughout the nanobelts (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1h</a>). Raman spectroscopy reveals the distinct scattering peaks of sodium titanate as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1i</a>. Notably, the Na─O─Ti bond manifests at ≈280 and 310 cm<sup>−1</sup>, and the Ti─O─Ti stretching bond in the edge-sharing TiO<sub>6</sub> structural unit is evidenced at ≈670 and 709 cm<sup>−1</sup>. The short Ti─O stretching vibration also occurs at ≈890 and 920 cm<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0015_R_d5642989e726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Raman Spectrosc.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, high-resolution X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) characterization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0001">1j</a>) shows two peaks located at 463.6 and 457.9&nbsp;eV in the Ti 2p scan, which can be assigned to Ti-O bonds of Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0007_R_d5642989e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively, suggesting the tetravalent nature of Ti in the 1D SCNs.</p>
         
         <p>In situ TEM observations were conducted to gain insight into the structural stability and morphology changes of the 1D SCNs during electrochemical operation. The experimental setup for the in situ observations is schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0002">2a</a>. The TEM image of the 1D SCNs during the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and the dynamic video showing the sodiation process are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0002">2b</a> and Video&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. A significant change was observed upon sodiation, with the reaction front moving rapidly forward (from right to left) along the longitudinal direction of the nanobelt. Additionally, the width of the 1D SCNs slightly expanded. These observations suggest that Na<sup>+</sup> ions mainly intercalate between the (001) lattice planes. Moreover, the well-defined SAED patterns captured during the sodiation process reveal that the 1D SCN could maintain its original crystalline order after Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400929-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/57adf350-040a-428b-9b02-28af57ee6329/aenm202400929-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/57adf350-040a-428b-9b02-28af57ee6329/aenm202400929-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c0d8171a-e185-4269-ae76-29d49a59a1a5/aenm202400929-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/57adf350-040a-428b-9b02-28af57ee6329/aenm202400929-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400929-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400929" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ TEM characterization of the 1D SCNs. a) Schematical illustration of the in situ TEM experimental setup. b) In situ observation of the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into 1D SCNs. c–h) Time lapse TEM images at different charge/discharge stages. i–k) In situ mechanical testing of the 1D SCNs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further investigate the electrochemo-mechanical stability of the 1D SCNs, time-lapse in situ TEM images at different charge/discharge stages were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0002">2c–h</a> and Video&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). Before the in situ observation, the 1D SCNs were completely sodiated. The in situ observation was then recorded, capturing the desodiation (Na<sup>+</sup> ion de-intercalation) process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0002">2c–e</a>) and the subsequent sodiation (Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation) process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0002">2f–h</a>). It was found that the 1D SCNs retained their structural integrity without fracturing throughout the whole electrochemical process. During desodiation, the SCN width remained nearly unchanged in the early stage (from 0–200 s). However, during later-stage desodiation (200-480 s), a slight decrease in the SCN width was observed due to the removal of Na<sup>+</sup> ions from the nanobelts. During subsequent sodiation, the width gradually increased, suggesting that the Na<sup>+</sup> can be reversibly intercalated into and de-intercalated out of the 1D SCNs.</p>
         
         <p>The mechanical performance of the 1D SCNs was further characterized using in situ TEM with loading strain and subsequent strain release as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0002">2i–k</a> and Video&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). When subjected to a mechanical load, the 1D SCNs underwent a 90° bending deformation without signs of fracturing. After releasing the mechanical load, the 1D SCNs immediately recovered, indicating no plastic deformation occurred during loading. Such robust mechanical performance is beneficial for managing the mechanical stress associated with the repetitive Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/de-intercalation, facilitating electrochemical stability over long-term cycling. The excellent mechanical performance also suggests the potential application of the 1D SCNs in fabricating flexible electrodes for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance of the 1D SCNs, we prepared a flexible film electrode composed of 1D SCNs and carbon nanotubes (denoted as SCNs/CNTs) through vacuum filtration, with the 1D SCNs and CNTs in a weight ratio of 9:1. More details about the fabrication of the SCNs/CNTs film are presented in the Experimental Section in the Supporting Information. The addition of CNTs facilitates electron conduction within the electrode, as confirmed by the significantly improved electronic conductivity, which increased from 4 × 10<sup>−7</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup> for the 1D SCNs to 2.4 × 10<sup>−2</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup> for the SCNs/CNTs, as determined by four-point probe measurements. The cross-sectional SEM image of the film reveals a thickness of ≈25&nbsp;µm, as shown in <b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3a</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a> (Supporting Information). Additionally, the SCNs/CNTs film exhibits high flexibility, maintaining structural integrity without cracking even after a 180° bend, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b,c</a> (Supporting Information). The SCNs/CNTs electrodes were cut into discs with a mass loading of ≈3.03&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and directly used as the working electrode for electrochemical performance characterizations.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400929-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/084d48f8-bdba-48e5-b39d-274a4e4b6463/aenm202400929-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/084d48f8-bdba-48e5-b39d-274a4e4b6463/aenm202400929-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c3b31e85-060e-4d5f-ab27-85ddf5b6352f/aenm202400929-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/084d48f8-bdba-48e5-b39d-274a4e4b6463/aenm202400929-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400929-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400929" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characterizations. a) Cross-sectional SEM image of the SCNs/CNTs film electrode. b) Typical charge/discharge profiles at 0.1 C and c) corresponding <i>dQ/dV</i> curves of the SCNs/CNTs. d) Rate capability. e) The analysis of the relationship between the CV peak current and the scan rates and f) separation of the capacitive contribution at the scan rate of 0.7&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> of the SCNs/CNTs. g) GITT profiles for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient analysis of the SCNs/CNTs. Cycling performance at 0.2 C (h), 1 C (i), and 20 C (j). Rate capability (k) and cycling performance at 1 C (l) at 0&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The charge/discharge curves of the initial two cycles of the film electrode at 0.1 C, along with their corresponding calculated differential capacity analysis are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3b,c</a>. It can be seen that the voltage-capacity profiles contain a pair of discharge/charge plateaus at ≈0.30/0.45&nbsp;V in both cycles, attributing to the reversible intercalation/de-intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> accompanied by the redox couple of Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0016_R_d5642989e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such a low redox potential plateau holds promise in enhancing the energy density of SIBs and helps avoid dangerous sodium plating, thus ensuring high safety. A notable sloping region appears above the plateau region within the voltage range of 0.35 to 0.5&nbsp;V (centered at ≈0.48&nbsp;V) in the initial discharge process. This region, which disappeared in the subsequent cycles, is attributed to side reactions of electrolyte reduction and forming a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0016_R_d5642989e896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These side reactions lead to a low Columbic efficiency in the initial cycle. In contrast, a reversible capacity of 228.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained in the second cycle. To determine the capacity contribution of the CNTs in the SCNs/CNTs film electrode, the CNTs film electrode was prepared, and its Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance was evaluated, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). The CNTs electrode delivered a capacity of ≈90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C. Therefore, within the SCNs/CNTs film electrode, the contribution of CNTs to the total capacity was estimated to be ≈9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, considering the 10 wt% CNTs in the electrode. For further comparison, NaTi<sub>1.25</sub>O<sub>3</sub> with an irregular, large (≈100&nbsp;nm) particle morphology (denoted as irNTO) was synthesized using a solid-state method as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a–c</a> (Supporting Information). The irNTO shows voltage profiles similar to those of the SCNs/CNTs but with a markedly lower capacity.</p>
         
         <p>The SCNs/CNTs film electrode exhibited excellent rate capability as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3d</a>. With a tenfold increase in the current density from 0.2 to 2 C, a reversible capacity of ≈150 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was retained. Remarkably, when the current density was further increased to 20 C, the film electrode delivered a capacity of ≈100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at an ultrahigh current rate of 30 C, the film electrode could be fully charged or discharged in one minute, achieving an average capacity of ≈80 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. This highlights the ultrafast Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage kinetics. In contrast, the irNTO demonstrated an inferior rate capability, achieving capacities of ≈85, 58, and 35 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1, 2, and 5 C, respectively. These capacities are significantly lower than those delivered by the SCNs/CNTs.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To understand the sodiation kinetics of the SCNs/CNTs, sweep-rate-dependent cyclic voltammetry (CV) analysis was conducted as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). The peak current (<i>i</i>) of the oxidation/reduction potential peaks and the scan rate (<i>v</i>) follow a classic relationship Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202400929-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202400929-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7c90a7cf-dc96-4b4e-8973-18230ffba91a/aenm202400929-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202400929:aenm202400929-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202400929-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mi>b</mi>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = a{{v}^b}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are parameters that can be calculated from the log(<i>i</i>) versus log(<i>v</i>) plots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0017_R_d5642989e979" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Nat. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The 1D SCNs exhibit calculated <i>b</i> values of 0.81 and 0.80 for cathodic and anodic peaks, respectively, indicating an intercalation pseudocapacitive charge storage mechanism. This intercalation pseudocapacitive mechanism combines a mixture of capacitor-like and diffusion-controlled responses, allowing Na<sup>+</sup> ions to be rapidly intercalated and de-intercalated into and out of the 1D SCNs during the redox processes. A qualitative understanding of the charge storage process can be obtained by separating the current response at a given potential into two contributions, i.e., capacitor-like (<i>k<sub>1</sub>v</i>) response and diffusion-controlled process (<i>k<sub>2</sub>v<sup>−1/2</sup></i>), following Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202400929-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202400929-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a707a550-f9f0-425c-a10d-dcbcb4660a1a/aenm202400929-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202400929:aenm202400929-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202400929-math-0002.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>

                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>

                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                                 <mo>/</mo>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </mrow>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = {{k}_1}v + {{k}_2}{{v}^{1/2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where <i>k<sub>1</sub></i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub></i> are constants.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0017_R_d5642989e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Nat. Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0018_R_d5642989e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The quantified results show that up to 72% of the total stored charge in the SCNs/CNTs film electrode can be assigned to a capacitor-like response at a sweep rate of 0.7&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3f</a>), resulting in a favorable Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage kinetics. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity in the SCNs/CNTs film electrode was investigated using galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) experiments as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3g</a>. During the test, a constant current density of 0.05 C was applied for 10&nbsp;min, followed by holding at an open-circuit voltage for 2&nbsp;h to reach a quasi-equilibrium potential. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients were calculated according to the following Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202400929-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202400929-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/29670e41-eb00-4af7-8aa5-69cfaefb1661/aenm202400929-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202400929:aenm202400929-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202400929-math-0003.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>N</mi>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>a</mi>
                                    <mo>+</mo>
                                 </msup>
                              </mrow>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mfrac>
                              <mn>4</mn>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>π</mi>
                                 <mi>τ</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfrac>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <msup>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>n</mi>
                                          <mi>m</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>V</mi>
                                          <mi>m</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mi>A</mi>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                           <msup>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>E</mi>
                                          <mi>s</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>E</mi>
                                          <mi>τ</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{D}_{N{{a}^ + }}} = \frac{4}{{\pi \tau }}\ {{\left( {\frac{{{{n}_m}{{V}_m}}}{A}} \right)}^2}{{\left( {\frac{{\Delta {{E}_s}}}{{\Delta {{E}_\tau }}}} \right)}^2}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>in which <i>τ</i> is the duration of the current pulse; <i>n<sub>m</sub></i> is the number of moles of electrode materials; <i>V<sub>m</sub></i> is the molar volume of the electrode materials; <i>A</i> is the electrochemically active area; Δ<i>E<sub>s</sub></i> is the steady-state voltage change due to the current pulse; and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> is the voltage change during the constant current pulse, eliminating the <i>iR</i> drop.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0019_R_d5642989e1136" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Phys. Chem. C.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More details about the GITT calculations and the parameters are presented in Note&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). It was found that the overpotentials in the plateau regions were relatively small, and the transient voltage profiles were in good agreement with the equilibrium curves, suggesting that the sodiation/desodiation processes are kinetically favorable. The calculated results show that the SCNs/CNTs film electrode presents an overall Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in the range of 10<sup>−8</sup>–10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, during the electrochemical processes, indicating a fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion due to the unique morphology and structural properties of the 1D SCNs.</div>
         
         <p>In addition to the excellent rate capability, the SCNs/CNTs film electrode shows remarkable cycling stability. At 0.2 C, the SCNs/CNTs showed stable cycling without significant capacity fading with a capacity of 183.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> retained after 100 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3h</a>). In contrast, the irNTO demonstrated a rapid capacity decay, maintaining only 63.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles at 0.2 C. To further evaluate the impact of CNTs on performance, the SCN film electrode without CNTs showed a significantly lower capacity and faster capacity fading (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information) due to the poor electronic conductivity of the 1D SCNs. For a more direct comparison, we also prepared an irNTO electrode without adding CNTs or any conductive agents. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8d,e</a> (Supporting Information), this electrode performed significantly worse than both the SCNs and the SCNs/CNTs film electrode. Therefore, the addition of CNTs is crucial in providing a conductive network that facilitates efficient electron transport throughout the electrode, which, in turn, allows the structural merits of the 1D SCNs to be fully utilized, thereby improving the electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>Moreover, the SCNs/CNTs exhibited ultralong-term cycling stability at 1 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3i</a>), delivering a reversible capacity of 163.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a capacity retention of 93%, with a high Columbic efficiency of ≈100% for 1000 cycles. Whereas the irNTO only exhibited a capacity of 54.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 81% over 500 cycles. Meanwhile, excellent cycling capability was also observed at higher current densities, showing 88% capacity retention over 5000 cycles at 20 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3j</a>). Even when the cycling rate is further increased to 30 C, the SCNs/CNTs preserve excellent reversibility without any remarkable capacity decay (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The excellent electrochemical performance of the SCNs/CNTs is superior to the results reported in the literature on sodium titanate anodes, as shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Encouragingly, the SCNs/CNTs also demonstrate good electrochemical reversibility at a low temperature of 0&nbsp;°C as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3k</a>, delivering specific capacities of ≈180 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C, 150 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C, 130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C, 110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C, and 50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C, respectively. Notably, the discharge capacities recovered to their initial values well when the current density was returned to 0.2 C. When cycled at 1 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0003">3l</a>), a reversible capacity of 140 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was maintained with Columbic efficiency of 100% and a capacity ratio of up to 85% over 550 cycles, demonstrating superior low-temperature cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the practical applicability viability of the SCNs/CNTs, a pouch cell was assembled using the SCNs/CNTs film electrode as the anode and a Prussian blue analog of Na<sub>1.53</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]·4.2H<sub>2</sub>O (denoted as PB) as the cathode, as schematically shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0004">4a</a>. The SCNs/CNTs film electrode and PB cathode were pre-cycled before the pouch cell assembly to compensate for the consumption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions resulting from the initial irreversible capacity loss. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0004">4b</a> shows the typical charge/discharge profiles of the PB cathode in a half cell with Na metal as anode. The PB cathode shows a long voltage plateau at ≈3.0&nbsp;V and delivers a reversible capacity of 98.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C in the voltage range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V. As a proof-of-concept, the rated capacity of the PB//SCNs/CNTs pouch cell was designed to be 1 mAh. The PB//SCNs/CNTs pouch cell displayed a remarkable voltage plateau at ≈2.5&nbsp;V derived from the voltage differences between the PB cathode and 1D SCNs anode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0004">4c</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0004">4d</a> presents the cycling performance of the PB//SCNs/CNTs pouch cell at 0.1 C, revealing that the PB//SCNs/CNTs pouch cell can achieve stable cycling. The cell maintained a capacity retention of 92% and a Coulombic efficiency of ≈99% over 150 cycles, achieving a low capacity fading ratio of 0.05% per cycle. The excellent performance of the PB//SCNs/CNTs pouch cell demonstrates the potential application of the 1D SCNs electrode in practical SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400929-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/22450990-c7f1-4aae-9cd0-0949aa798123/aenm202400929-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/22450990-c7f1-4aae-9cd0-0949aa798123/aenm202400929-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2c8807dd-429b-4a84-8c76-c4f097706bf7/aenm202400929-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/22450990-c7f1-4aae-9cd0-0949aa798123/aenm202400929-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400929-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400929" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Battery performance characterization in a pouch cell. a) Schematic illustration of the pouch cell assembled of the SCNs/CNTs film anode and PB cathode. b) Charge/discharge profiles of the PB cathode at 0.1 C. c) Charge/discharge profiles of the PB//SCNs/CNTs pouch cell. d) Cycling performance of the PB//SCNs/CNTs pouch cell at 0.1 C. The inset is the photo of the PB//SCNs/CNTs pouch cell demo.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed to understand the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation dynamics and to determine the localized migration energy barriers in the SCNs/CNTs film electrode. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a> (Supporting Information) shows the solid-state diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> in a bulk structure of sodium titanate, where the intercalated Na<sup>+</sup> ions occupy the Na2 site and are surrounded by four nearest neighbor Na<sup>+</sup> ions (Na1 site) in the pristine structure. The Coulomb interaction between the Na1 and Na2 impedes the direct diffusion of Na2 to the Na2′ site. Instead, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion followed a vacancy-hopping mechanism as shown in Video&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). The Coulomb repulsion between Na1 and Na2 ions induces the migration of one Na1 ion to occupy the neighboring Na2′ site, resulting in a Na1 vacancy. Subsequently, the Na2 ion fills the Na1 vacancy. The diffusion process can be expressed as Na2 + Na1 → Na2 + Na1<sub>vacancy</sub> + Na2′ → Na1 + Na2′. The activation energy change associated with the diffusion path is plotted in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a> (Supporting Information), showing that the lowest energy barrier is calculated to be 0.84&nbsp;eV along the [010] direction. However, when coupled with the CNTs, the energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion is further reduced as illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0005">5a–c</a> and Video&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). The exposed edge sites of the (001) plane offer abundant active sites, facilitating the formation of more feasible paths and thereby providing energetically favorable routes for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. The extensively exposed planes of the 1D SCNs ensure close contact with the CNTs, promoting efficient electron conduction at the atomic interface, thereby further facilitating the rapid diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Consequently, Na<sup>+</sup> ions demonstrate direct diffusion along the [010] direction at the SCNs/CNTs interface, resulting in a shortened diffusion path. This diffusion mechanism significantly reduces the activation energy barrier of 0.65&nbsp;eV for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, contributing to fast reaction kinetics.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400929-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5f00b7fb-f85c-4198-ac39-86f2f68c8194/aenm202400929-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5f00b7fb-f85c-4198-ac39-86f2f68c8194/aenm202400929-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/81ea19dd-767b-4d68-9395-b98fa959a651/aenm202400929-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5f00b7fb-f85c-4198-ac39-86f2f68c8194/aenm202400929-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400929-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400929" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Theoretical structural mode of the interface contact between the 1D SCNs and CNTs. b) Illustration of the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion along the [010] direction and c) corresponding migration energy of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion at the SCNs/CNTs atomic interface. d) In situ synchrotron XRD analysis of the 1D SCNs during initial charge/discharge processes. Left: XRD patterns of the (001) diffraction peaks. Middle: charge/discharge profile. Right: 3D intensity mapping of the (001) diffraction peaks. e) Calculated lattice parameters of the selected XRD patterns during the discharge/charge process.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The crystal structure evolution of the 1D SCNs during the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and de-intercalation was investigated using in situ synchrotron XRD as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0005">5d</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). The 1D SCNs show a reversible structural evolution during the charge/discharge processes as evidenced by the reversible changes in the position and intensity of the diffraction peaks. At the beginning of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation, no new peaks beyond the 1D SCNs structure were observed. Notably, the main diffraction peak of (001) experienced a slight variation in their position from initial discharge until 0.4&nbsp;V; however, the shift was small since only limited Na<sup>+</sup> ions were inserted into the 1D SCNs in this stage. In the following plateau process (centered at ≈0.30&nbsp;V), continued Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation led to the (001) peak shifting to lower two theta angles, mainly due to the intensive Coulomb repulsion between the Na<sup>+</sup> and TiO<sub>6</sub> layers in the crystal structure. The shift of the (001) peak is associated with a <i>d</i>-spacing expansion and an increase in the lattice parameter <i>c</i>, suggesting that the 1D SCNs underwent a lattice volume change. Similarly, theta position changes were observed in the (20-2) and (020) peaks, indicating slight increases in the <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> lattice parameters. The lattice parameters of the selected in situ XRD patterns were calculated as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0005">5e</a>. Both parameters show an increasing trend during the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation process and a decrease during the following Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation process. Nevertheless, the unit-cell volume change before and after sodium intercalation is only 2.1%, demonstrating a low-strain characteristic. Additionally, some secondary peaks appear to the right of the (001) peaks at voltages below 0.08&nbsp;V. These new peaks are associated with forming a superstructural ordering resulting from changes in the oxygen stacking sequence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0014_R_d5642989e1369" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Acta Crystallogr. B Adv. Sci.">14</a>, <a href="#aenm202400929-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400929-bib-0016_R_d5642989e1369" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Detailed calculations and comparisons of the lattice parameters at 0.08 and 0.01&nbsp;V (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) show that this new phase exhibits lattice parameters very similar to those of the sodiated 1D SCNs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400929-fig-0005">5e</a>), indicating a high structural matching between the phases. Notably, even after fully discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V, the unit-cell expansion is calculated to be only 0.6% compared to 0.08&nbsp;V, indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> storage at this stage does not induce significant structural changes. This is likely due to a limited number of Na<sup>+</sup> ions stored that brings minimal structural impact, as evidenced by a relatively low capacity achieved in this voltage range. Furthermore, the cycling performance of the SCNs/CNTs composite, with 93% capacity retention after 1000 cycles at 1C, supports that the new phase formation and Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation do not cause significant structural changes affecting electrochemical performance. Moreover, these secondary peaks disappeared during the subsequent charge process with Na<sup>+</sup> de-intercalation. Similar reversible structural changes were observed during the subsequent cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, the excellent structural reversibility and unique low-strain properties ensure the outstanding long-cycle performance of the 1D SCNs, as demonstrated by over 5000 cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400929-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400929-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have prepared 1D single-crystal nanobelts, comprising self-assembled monolayer nanobelts, which are efficient anode materials for SIBs. Benefiting from the unique structural characteristics of the 1D SCNs, including their large interlayer distance, abundant active sites, high mechanical strength, 1D self-assembly configuration, and favorable interface electrochemical kinetics; the resulting SCNs/CNTs film electrodes show excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance with highly reversible capacity, fast charge/discharge capability, and long cycling stability. In situ characterizations reveal the robust mechanical strength of the 1D single-crystal configuration, rendering a low-strain characteristic and enabling an exceptionally long-term cycling stability of over 5000 cycles. A pouch cell assembled with a 1D single-crystal nanobelt anode shows stable cycling with a low capacity fading ratio of ≈0.05% per cycle. We anticipate these findings will pave a new way for developing electrode materials for high-performance batteries.</p>
         </section>
